                            <div class="player-info-box-header team-nationals"><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>Howie Kendrick</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team"><a href="/teams/nationals">Washington Nationals</a></div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 34</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: R/R</div>

        <div class="player-info-box-pos">2B</div></div>

            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>The good news is that Kendrick did not get injured in 2009.  The bad news is that he was playing so poorly the Angels sent him to the minors the second week in June.  But when he returned on the Fourth of July, Kendrick was a different hitter.  In his final 54 games of the season, he batted .351/.387/.532 over 199 plate appearances.  Kendrick still does not walk much, but his 5.1% BB rate was the highest of his Major League career.  He made a big improvement in laying off pitches outside the zone.  Kendrick’s 31.4% O-Swing% was the lowest of his career and that led to a 79.4% Contact%, a personal best.  And not only did Kendrick make better contact, he also hit with more authority, as his .152 ISO topped his previous high by 21 points.<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>Most fantasy owners have been burned by Kendrick at some point or another the past three years, and his brutal start ensured that his final 2009 numbers were nothing special. Combine that with breakout seasons last year from second basemen like Asdrubal Cabrera, Aaron Hill and Ben Zobrist, Kendrick figures to be an afterthought on Draft Day.  And that is the recipe for a nice sleeper pick.  Kendrick is a lifetime .302 hitter in 1,319 at-bats in the Majors, so we know he is going to put up a good average.  The big question is how much of the power gains Kendrick can retain in 2010.  He posted a 12.2% HR/FB mark last year, nearly three times his 2008 mark.  If Kendrick can provide 15 homers and 80 RBI to go along with a .300 average, he will be a $13-15 player yet be available in the second half of most drafts. (Brian Joura)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Over 2000 plate appearances into his career, the bloom is off the Kendrick rose. It's not that he's a terrible player -- he has a little power (.131 career ISO, .152 peak ISO in 2009), speed (47 career stolen bases, 5.2 career speed score), and the ability to put up decent batting averages (.295 career) -- but maybe he's not the Future Batting Champ that has been predicted of him. Or, maybe he could still win a batting title someday (he makes decent contact with a career 17% strikeout rate, and his speed has led to a career .339 BABIP), but the rest of his package still mutes his overall upside. Last year saw a step back from 2009 in ISO (.152 to .128) and speed (6.0 to 4.9 speed score), but he only just turned 27. A bounceback year would see him crack double-digits in home runs and strikeouts while adding a .300 batting average. Acceptable, but not exciting. (Eno Sarris)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>"Acceptable, but not exciting" may not have been the description Kendrick supporters thought he'd garner in the peak of his career, but his lack of game-changing speed or power leaves him as a mostly one-category performer. And that category (batting average) is a flighty one.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>When Kendrick was originally called up by the Angels in 2006, many regarded him as a future batting title winner, but he has proven to us that he will never fulfill that potential in his 2500 MLB at bats. However, Kendrick showed some added potential in 2011, as he hit 18 balls out of the ballpark in only 140 games on the field. he also raised his line drive percentage last season and maintained a respectable stolen base total. Kendrick is in his prime years for power production, but if last year is any indication, added pop could lead to increased strikeouts for the 28-year-old. Kendrick’s value is going to come from producing in multiple categories, and if you think he can hit .280 while hitting 15 homers and stealing 15 bases, he’s a pretty safe pick to be your second baseman. (Zach Sanders)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Kendrick added extra power to his game last year at the expense of a worse strikeout rate. The 28-year-old is a pretty safe fantasy bet in 2012, and he may still have some upside left in him.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>After a 2011 power surge, Howie Kendrick returned to his old ways in 2012. Even with the dip in power production, Kendrick’s 2012 still made him a seven-dollar player in standard mixed leagues. At this point, it seems like he is what he is, and no changes are likely on the horizon; Kendrick will hit around .285 with 10 homers and 15 steals, and while that’s nothing to sneeze at, it’s also not enough to get owners excited about drafting him. If Kendrick can drop his strikeouts by a couple percentage points, he’ll have a much better chance of raising his batting average and be worthy of starting in mixed leagues. Pay a few bucks for Kendrick, but make sure he’s not the only second baseman on your mixed-league roster. (Zach Sanders)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Howie Kendrick will give you a good batting average, but not a whole lot else. He is worthy of a few of your dollars in mixed leagues.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>There are players you target, there are plan B players, and then there are the fallback options. I see Howie Kendrick as the quintessential fallback for your second base strategy. The great thing about Kendrick is he provides just enough across the board to be useful. He has a career .292/.329/.429 slash line, and after eight seasons and almost 4,000 plate appearances, you can probably hang your hat on him reproducing something in that range going into 2014. Kendrick should break through to double digit home runs and steals, although it might take him until September to get there. Depending on where he hits in the lineup, he ought to provide a decent number of runs and RBI as well. He's certainly not an impact fantasy player, but he's not a black hole in any particular category and he won't cost but a few bucks or late round flier on draft day. He's kind of a Martin Prado prototype with a dearth of hype. (<a href="https://twitter.com/michaelcbarr">Michael Barr</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Howie Kendrick is a handy little player to either stash on your bench in case disaster strikes your starting second baseman, or he is a decent play should you have a middle infield spot to fill. He doesn't do anything particularly great, but he doesn't do anything poorly in standard roto formats. He's better than you thought he was, and yet still not a player to target on draft day. If .280, 12 home runs, 12 stolen bases, 65-75 runs and RBI floats your boat, Kendrick is your guy.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Does anybody remember when Kendrick was a perennial injury risk? The narrative used to be that Kendrick was a batting champion in the making -- he just couldn't stay on the field. The 31-year-old has managed nearly 3,000 plate appearances in the last five seasons, but he's also never put up a .300 average in a full season. Both narratives are dead and gone. Instead, look to the new Dodger for decent production at a shallow fantasy position. While no batting champ, he hits for a good average, reaches base at a reasonable rate, and can chip in a few home runs and steals. Last season was a career year with the Angels, and he should benefit from another strong supporting cast. Kendrick received a lot of opportunities to hit in the middle of the Angels lineup. Unfortunately, those same chances might not exist with the Dodgers. (Brad Johnson)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Kendrick was traded from the Angels to the Dodgers over the offseason. His new home should allow him to generate plenty of fantasy value as he prepares to enter real world free agency.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Howie Kendrick may have changed addresses in southern California last year when he moved from the Angels to the Dodgers, but the rate stats for the second baseman remained virtually the same. His .295/.336/.409 slash line reads like a carbon copy of his career .293/.333/.423 mark, even if, at 32 years old, age is starting to eat away at the things that made Kendrick a valued keystone option for years. That’s now two out of the last three years in which he’s stolen just six bases, two straight seasons in which he’s failed to reach double digits in home runs, and his 117 games were his fewest since 2009. Much of the blame can be hanged on a strained left hamstring that cost him more than five weeks in the second half and continued to bother him upon his return in September, when he posted a mere .622 on-base plus slugging percentage through the end of the season. Now that he's back at home at the top of a good lineup in Los Angeles, he should be good for a repeat of his slash lines. But even if we can expect the usual Kendrick production in rate stats for 2016 — a good-not-great batting average and a modest on-base percentage — we should also expect dwindling counting stats, with single-digit showings in steals and home runs. That doesn’t eliminate Kendrick’s fantasy value, but it probably relegates him just outside starter status in standard mixed leagues and perhaps makes him more of a high-end middle infield option. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Karl_de_Vries">Karl de Vries</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Howie Kendrick still does a lot well, but he’s also starting to show signs of wear at age 32. There’s something comforting about Kendrick’s consistent rate production, but he’s becoming less helpful as a steals and power contributor at second base, making him more of a single-category producer.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>One of the more consistent players of the last decade saw his production fall dramatically in 2016. Kendrick could easily be penciled in for .290/.330/.420 with nearly double-digit home runs and almost 30 doubles. Injuries, defensive uncertainty and attrition played a factor in his almost season-long slump. He finished with full-season-lows in batting average, slugging percentage, wRC+ and home runs. Through June 12, Kendrick had a paltry .212/.276/.284 triple slash with six extra base hits and a 53 wRC+ -- 55 points off his career-average through 2015. He rebounded and performed closer to his true capabilities the rest of the way (.274/.352/.409, 110 wRC+), which helped him salvage a tumultuous campaign. Kendrick did improve his walk rate to an above-average 9.5 percent. That was 2.1 percent higher than at any other time of his career. It probably isn't sustainable, though. Defensively, Kendrick had dabbled at first- and third base as well as left field, but he had been primarily a (good) second baseman heading into 2016. He played just 210 1/3 innings at second base, with most of his playing time coming in left field (675 1/3 innings). The metrics showed he was average out there, while the eye test said he was closer to a below-average left fielder, which is where he's going to get most of his playing time this season. His trade to Philadelphia could help him regain some of his value, as he didn't really have a spot in Los Angeles in 2017.<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Kendrick's consistency had been lauded in MLB circles for a decade. At some point, he was bound to have a down season, and 2016 was that. He had career-worst numbers in a lot of categories and as a 33-year-old, his best days are behind him. He'll have a chance to rejuvenate his career in Philly before, probably, being dealt at the trade deadline.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

Much of the blame can be hanged on a strained left hamstring that cost him more than five weeks in the second half and continued to bother him upon his return in September, when he posted a mere .622 on-base plus slugging percentage through the end of the season. Now that he's back at home at the top of a good lineup in Los Angeles, he should be good for a repeat of his slash lines. But even if we can expect the usual Kendrick production in rate stats for 2016 — a good-not-great batting average and a modest on-base percentage — we should also expect dwindling counting stats, with single-digit showings in steals and home runs. That doesn’t eliminate Kendrick’s fantasy value, but it probably relegates him just outside starter status in standard mixed leagues and perhaps makes him more of a high-end middle infield option.
